For a moment she looked less like a suspect than a woman holding a door closed with her whole body while something on the other side pressed back.

Chapter 6: The Boy in the Quarry

June Pritchard refused to tell the story in Nora Bell's parlor.

"Walls remember in this house," she said, and walked out before either woman could answer.

Mara followed her through the dining room, where breakfast had become theater staged by people pretending not to watch the actors leave. Caleb stood near the sideboard with a toolbox in one hand and a scone in the other. He raised his eyebrows. Mara shook her head once. Not now.

Outside, the air had sharpened. The storm's last clouds dragged east over the lake, and sunlight fell in narrow, unreliable bands across the square. June descended the inn steps slowly, refusing assistance with a dignity that made assistance feel like an accusation. She crossed to a bench beside the Civil War monument and sat facing St. Bartholomew's.

"If this is about Daniel Bell," Mara said, "Sheriff Harrow needs to hear it."

"Dina Harrow hears what can be entered on forms. This is not that yet."

"It will have to become that."

"Most true things do, after they have been damaged into usefulness."

Mara sat beside her. June smelled faintly of lavender and old paper. Up close, the retired teacher looked smaller than memory but not softer. Her eyes remained the pale, assessing blue that had once made seventh graders confess to chewing gum before she asked.

"Tell me," Mara said.

June tapped her cane against the wet pavement. "In July of 1998, Bellweather was celebrating the new marina wing, pretending the mill closure hadn't frightened everyone, and allowing Alistair Bell to behave as if the town were a table he owned because, in practical terms, he owned enough chairs. Daniel came home from college with opinions. Expensive opinions. He had discovered injustice at Bowdoin and returned to distribute it locally."

"What kind of injustice?"

"Land, mostly. The quarry. The mill cottages. Old leases the Bell family had written when desperate people signed anything."

"Daniel was going to expose his father?"

"Daniel was nineteen. He was going to expose everyone, loudly, after two drinks."

That sounded like a boy, not a martyr. Mara preferred it. Real victims were rarely arranged for easy reverence.

"Who was with him the night he drowned?"

June looked across the square. "Nora. Caleb Rusk. Lydia Frost, though she was Lydia March then. Thomas Keene, before seminary. Your cousin Peter for part of the evening. Owen Vale, younger than the rest and desperate to be included. And three summer boys from the marina whose names changed depending on who was telling it."

"My cousin Peter never mentioned being there."

"Peter has made a full life of not mentioning things."

Mara filed that away. Peter Ellison lived in Bangor now and sent Christmas cards featuring children Mara could not reliably name.

"And my mother?"

"Eleanor was not at the quarry that night. She arrived the next morning."

"Why?"

"Because Caleb came to her."

Mara turned. "Caleb?"

"He was seventeen. Frightened. Bleeding from the jaw. He told her Daniel had not drowned where they said he drowned."

The scar along Caleb's jaw reappeared in Mara's mind, pale against rain-dark stubble.

"What did he say happened?"

"I don't know."

"June."

"I don't. Eleanor did not tell me everything. Contrary to local belief, we were friends, not a two-woman intelligence agency."

"But you knew enough."

June's mouth tightened. "I knew Eleanor took her camera to the quarry. I knew Amos rang the bell once that afternoon, a single note he later claimed was for a funeral in Bar Harbor, which was nonsense. I knew Alistair Bell came to school two days later and asked Eleanor for the negatives from the yearbook darkroom."

"Did she give them?"

"She gave him negatives."

"Not the negatives."

"Your mother was a precise woman."

Mara looked toward the inn. Nora had come onto the porch and stood between two columns, watching them. Even at this distance her stillness was conspicuous.

"June, did Daniel's body come from the quarry?"

The teacher's hand stopped moving on the cane.

"A body came from the quarry."

"Was it Daniel?"

"Alistair identified him. Closed casket. Quick burial. The medical examiner was from county, overworked, and inclined to trust a father who owned half the tax base. Dental records were requested, then found unnecessary. That is what the file said."

"You read the file?"

"I read many files. People leave old women alone with cabinets."

Mara almost smiled. "Why didn't you push?"

June's expression hardened. "I did push. Eleanor pushed. Amos pushed in his way. Then Daniel's mother tried to kill herself, Caleb recanted whatever he had told Eleanor, Lydia left for nursing school, Nora became the daughter who remained, and Alistair Bell made it clear that anyone prolonging the matter was tormenting a grieving family. Small towns are not brave in groups. They are brave one person at a time, and then they punish the person for embarrassing them."

The bell tower rose above them, silent now. Yellow crime scene tape crossed the church doors. A gull landed on the monument and shrieked once, offended by history.

"What was in the photograph?" Mara asked.

June took a long breath. "I saw only a print, years later. Eleanor showed it to me after Alistair died. A boy standing near the quarry road at dawn on July fourth, wrapped in a blanket, face turned partly away."

"Daniel?"

"Perhaps."

"Perhaps?"

"It looked like him. It also looked like what grief wants when it refuses a coffin."

"Who else was in the photograph?"

"Amos, in the background. And someone beside him wearing a blue scarf."

Blue cloth in Amos's dead hand.

Mara stood too quickly. The square shifted, then steadied.

June looked up. "You see why I preferred not to say this in Nora's parlor."

"Who wore the scarf?"

"I don't know. The face was turned. But the scarf had white stitching along the edge."

Mara's pulse moved once, hard.

"Did Eleanor keep the original?"

"She said she kept insurance."

"Where?"

"If I knew, Mara, I would already have given it to Dina and enjoyed the rare pleasure of being useful before breakfast."

Mara looked toward Alder Street. Her mother's house stood out of sight behind maples and wet roofs. Someone had opened and resealed the envelope. Someone might already have the photograph. Or the copy. Or the wrong thing, because Eleanor had trusted precision more than fear.

June's voice softened. "Your mother did not tell you because she thought leaving would save you from Bellweather."

"Leaving didn't."

"No," June said. "It rarely does. It only changes the road back."

Across the square, Sheriff Harrow emerged from the church with Voss beside her. Harrow saw Mara with June, saw Mara's face, and began walking toward them with the quick, contained stride of a woman who knew bad news had found a new mouth.

Mara met her halfway.

"We need Caleb Rusk's 1998 statement," she said.

Harrow stopped. "There isn't one."

"There was."

"The file index doesn't list it."

"Then pull the original box."

"County is looking."

"Tell them to look harder."

Harrow glanced past her at June. "What did she tell you?"

"That Daniel Bell may have been alive the morning after he drowned."

Voss said something under his breath.

Harrow's face did not change, but Mara saw the moment the case widened past the tower, past Amos, past the clean boundaries everyone wanted. Murder in a church was terrible. Murder that reached back twenty-seven years was worse. It meant the town had been living over a cellar and calling it foundation.

"Caleb's at the inn," Mara said.

"I'll talk to him."

"So will I."

"No."

"He came to my mother in 1998."

Harrow's eyes sharpened. "Did he tell you that?"

"June did."

"Then June can put it in a statement, and you can go home."

Mara looked at the church, the inn, the square arranged around them like a jury. "Someone has already been in my mother's study. The envelope was opened and resealed. Whoever killed Amos knew Eleanor mattered. If Caleb knows why, waiting politely may cost you the next piece."

Voss looked at Harrow. "She's not wrong."

"Everyone in this town has decided to tell me that today," Harrow said.

But she was already turning toward the inn.

Caleb saw them coming through the dining room window. He set down his toolbox. For one second, his face was not confused, not curious, not innocent. It was the face of a man who had heard a bell he had been expecting for twenty-seven years.

Then he picked up his jacket and ran.

Chapter 7: Owen's Alibi

Caleb Rusk did not run like a guilty man in films. He ran like a man who knew the alleys.

He went through the Bell House kitchen, knocked a tray of biscuits from a server's hands, apologized as he moved, and shouldered open the back door into the service yard. Harrow followed first, fast for someone in duty boots. Voss went after her. Mara was three steps behind and gaining until the inn's cook backed into her path with a cast-iron skillet raised like a medieval weapon.

"Police," Mara said, then remembered she was not.

The cook did not lower the skillet. "You don't look like police."

"She's with me," Harrow shouted from outside.

That was not true in any official sense, but it moved the skillet.

Mara burst into the yard. Caleb had crossed the gravel lot and vaulted the low fence into the lane behind the inn. He slipped once on wet leaves, caught himself against a trash bin, and kept going toward the marina. His limp showed after the slip. Left knee, maybe old, maybe fresh. He had enough of a lead to choose his route and enough local knowledge to make that matter.

"Caleb!" Harrow shouted.

He did not look back.

The lane opened behind the row of shops facing the square. Delivery doors, propane tanks, stacked lobster traps, rain barrels, the bakery's exhaust fan breathing sugar and yeast into the cold air. Caleb cut left at the florist, then right behind the closed movie house, aiming for the narrow footpath that ran down to the docks. Mara knew it because she had used it at sixteen with a bottle of stolen peach schnapps and no capacity for regret.

"He's going to the water," she called.

Harrow swore and angled toward the street to intercept. Voss stayed on Caleb's line. Mara took the cut-through behind the old library, a muddy strip between a stone wall and a hedge, and came out near the harbormaster's shed just as Caleb reached the ramp.

The docks heaved on gray chop. Boats knocked against bumpers. Ropes creaked. Caleb sprinted past the fuel pump toward a work skiff tied at the end of the first float.

"Don't," Mara said.

He stopped with one foot in the skiff and one on the dock. For a second the lake rocked him between choices.

Mara held up both hands. She was breathing hard enough to taste metal. "If you take the boat, you become easier to misunderstand."

"I'm already understood wrong."

"Then help me correct it."

Harrow came onto the dock behind Mara with her hand on her sidearm but not drawing. Voss slowed near the ramp, giving the dock less weight. Caleb saw the geometry of capture form around him. He stepped back from the skiff.

"I didn't kill Amos," he said.

"No one asked yet," Harrow replied.

"Everyone asked. They just used their faces."

Harrow moved closer. "Why did you run?"

Caleb looked past them to town. "Because June talked."

"About 1998?"

His jaw worked around something old. "I was a kid."

"You were seventeen," Mara said.

"That's a kid when the adults are rich, drunk, and frightened."

Harrow took out her cuffs. "Caleb Rusk, you're being detained while I sort out whether you're a material witness, an idiot, or both."

"Dina."

"Hands."

He gave them to her. Not meekly, but without resistance. The click of cuffs sounded too loud over the water.

Mara watched his face. "What did you tell my mother?"

Harrow shot her a warning look, but Caleb answered before she could stop him.

"That Daniel walked out of the quarry."

The dock moved under them. A gull cried from a piling. Somewhere up the hill, a car horn gave one impatient bleat and stopped.

"Alive?" Voss asked.

Caleb laughed once, broken and airless. "People usually walk that way."

Harrow tightened her grip on his arm. "Where did he go?"

"I don't know."

"Who hurt your jaw?"

He looked at Mara. "Daniel."

That was not the answer she expected. It complicated the photograph, the scar, the boy wrapped in a blanket, Eleanor's hidden envelope. Caleb saw her thinking and shook his head.

"Not like that," he said. "He was scared. We all were."

"Start at the beginning," Harrow said.

"Not on a dock."

"You lost the right to choose setting when you ran."

"Dina, if you want me to say it once and say it clean, put me in a room. If you want half the marina to hear me turn a dead Bell boy into a living problem, keep talking here."

Harrow looked toward the bait shop. Three men had found urgent business near its window. Nora stood on the inn porch in the distance, no longer pretending not to watch. Reverend Keene had come out of the church and frozen at the steps.

"Fine," Harrow said. "Station."

They marched Caleb up from the docks in cuffs. Bellweather watched through curtains, windshields, shop glass, and the honest stare of people too old to bother with concealment. By the time they reached the square, Owen Vale was shouting on the courthouse steps.

Owen had inherited Amos's sharp nose and none of his restraint. He was thirty, narrow, and damp, with a cheap suit jacket over a T-shirt advertising a band Mara had never heard of. His eyes were red. A deputy blocked him from crossing toward the church.

"You can't keep me out," Owen said. "He was my uncle."

"It's a scene," Pike said.

"It's a church."

"Today it's a scene."

Owen saw Caleb in cuffs and pointed. "What, you got him now? Good. Ask him why Amos was going to meet him."

Caleb stopped.

Harrow turned slowly. "What did you say?"

Owen looked suddenly less certain of his performance. "Amos. He said he was meeting Rusk."

"When?"

"Last night."

Caleb said, "No."

"Don't lie," Owen snapped. "He wrote it down."

Mara moved closer. "Where?"

Owen swallowed. "On the pad by his phone. I saw it yesterday afternoon. 'Rusk after bell practice.'"

"You were in Amos's cottage yesterday?" Harrow asked.

"I visit."

"You told Pike you hadn't seen him in a week."

"I forgot."

"You forgot seeing your uncle the day before he was murdered?"

Owen's grief curdled into panic. "I was upset."

"Where were you during the bell ringing?"

"Home."

"Anyone with you?"

"No."

"Anyone see you?"

"No."

Harrow let the silence stretch.

Owen looked from her to Caleb to Mara. "I didn't kill him. I wanted money, all right? He said no. He always said no. But I didn't kill him."

"What money?"

"My money. Family money. He had no right to sit on everything like some saint with a bank account."

Caleb said quietly, "Owen, shut up."

"You shut up. You're the one he was meeting."

Harrow handed Caleb to Voss. "Take him inside. Pike, bring Owen."

"Am I arrested?" Owen asked.

"You are about to become very helpful."

The station occupied the first floor of the old courthouse, a brick building with bad plumbing and portraits of selectmen who looked uniformly disappointed in modernity. Harrow put Caleb in interview room one and Owen in interview room two. Mara remained in the hallway because that was as far as Harrow's patience extended.

Through the wall, Owen's voice rose and fell. "I told you, I was home. I drank. I slept. I woke up when the bell rang like everybody else."

Voss passed Mara with two cups of station coffee. "You're not interviewing anyone."

"I'm aware."

"You have the look."

"What look?"

"Like a locked door insulted you personally."

Despite herself, Mara accepted the coffee he offered. "Owen is too messy."

"Messy people kill people."

"Yes. Then they keep being messy at the scene. Amos's death had structure."

"Maybe Owen got lucky."

"Luck doesn't return ledgers to locked drawers."

Voss leaned against the opposite wall. "What about Caleb?"

Mara watched the closed interview room door. "Caleb ran from 1998, not from last night."

"That's generous."

"It's provisional."

In room two, Owen began to cry. The sound was angry and young and humiliating to hear through institutional drywall.

Harrow opened room one and stepped into the hall. "Mara."

Mara straightened.

"Caleb says he'll talk if you're present."

"No," Voss said immediately.

"I know," Harrow said.

"Then why are you telling her?"

Harrow looked tired enough to be honest. "Because he says the thing he has to say belongs partly to Eleanor Ellison, and I'm running out of ways to pretend this case isn't already using Mara's dead mother as a door."

Mara set down the coffee.

Voss frowned. "If she goes in, she becomes discoverable."

"I was always discoverable," Mara said.

Harrow pointed at her. "You speak only if I ask you to. You do not lead him. You do not rescue him. You do not decide the interview is yours because history hurt your feelings."

"Understood."

Inside, Caleb sat cuffed to the table, wet hair falling over his forehead. He looked up when Mara entered, and the relief in his face made her angry.

"Don't be relieved," she said.

Harrow shut the door. "Start with Daniel walking out of the quarry."

Caleb looked at the recorder on the table, then at Mara.

"He wasn't supposed to be in the water," he said. "That's the first lie. Daniel didn't drown swimming. He was running from us."

Chapter 8: Salt on the Steps

Caleb's statement did not become cleaner as it lengthened. It became more human, which was worse.

In 1998, he said, Daniel Bell had arrived at the quarry with a bottle of bourbon, a canvas satchel, and the righteous fury of a rich boy who had discovered his own inheritance was evidence. Nora had tried to calm him. Thomas Keene had tried to pray without calling it prayer. Lydia March had cried. Owen, fourteen and eager to be useful, had carried beer from a cooler and laughed whenever older people laughed.

Daniel wanted to show them documents from the satchel. Leases. Letters. A map of the mill cottages. Proof, he claimed, that Alistair Bell had been forcing families off land the Bell family no longer clearly owned.

"Was Amos there?" Harrow asked.

"Not at first," Caleb said.

"Eleanor?"

"No."

"Then who hit you?"

Caleb touched his jaw as if the old injury had begun hurting under attention. "Daniel. I grabbed the satchel. Nora asked me to. She said he was drunk and would ruin himself. I thought I was helping."

Mara heard the boy inside the man: eager, ashamed, still trying to be judged by the intention instead of the result.

"Daniel swung," Caleb continued. "Caught me with a ring. Split me open. I dropped the satchel. Papers went everywhere. The wind took some into the water."

"Then?"

"Then headlights came down the quarry road."

"Alistair Bell?"

Caleb nodded. "And Amos with him."

"Why Amos?"

"I don't know. Maybe Alistair brought him to witness Daniel being unreasonable. Maybe Amos followed. Amos did that. He appeared where record might be needed."

Harrow wrote without looking up. "What happened when Alistair arrived?"

"He and Daniel shouted. About land. About Daniel's mother. About someone named Ruth."

Mara leaned forward despite herself.

Harrow's pen stopped. "Ruth who?"

"I don't know. Daniel said, 'Ask Ruth what you bought with her silence.' Alistair hit him."

The room held still.

"Fist?" Voss asked.

"Backhand. Hard. Daniel fell. Hit a rock, I think. There was blood near his ear. He got up, but wrong. Staggering. Alistair reached for him, and Daniel ran toward the upper path."

"Not the water."

"No. Away from it."

"Then why did people say he drowned?"

Caleb closed his eyes. "Because later we heard a splash."

It took twenty minutes to draw the rest from him. Daniel had run into the trees above the quarry. Alistair ordered everyone to stay put, then went after him. Nora followed her father. Amos remained near the water, collecting papers with shaking hands. Thomas Keene prayed openly now. Lydia wrapped Caleb's jaw with a scarf, blue with white stitching, because he was bleeding on his shirt. Owen cried and denied crying.

Then came a shout. Then the splash.

Alistair returned alone, soaked to the waist, and said Daniel had fallen from the high ledge into the flooded quarry pit. The water was black. The storm had come in. No one could see him. No one should risk another life.

"Did you believe him?" Harrow asked.

"I was seventeen," Caleb said. "I believed the loudest adult."

But later, near dawn, Caleb walked back toward the quarry because guilt was a rope and he was already tied to it. He saw Daniel on Quarry Road, alive, wrapped in an old horse blanket from Amos's truck, arguing with Amos and a woman whose face Caleb could not see.

"The blue scarf?" Mara asked.

Harrow glared at her, but Caleb answered.

"No. Lydia still had the scarf. She used it on my jaw. The woman with Amos wore a raincoat with the hood up. I thought it was Nora at first. Then I saw Nora's car pass me coming from town."

"Did Daniel see you?"

"Yes. He came at me. Grabbed my shirt. He said, 'Tell Eleanor the bell lied.' I didn't know what that meant. Then Amos pushed him away and told me to go home."

"Why Eleanor?" Voss asked.

Caleb looked at Mara. "She ran the school paper. Darkroom. Yearbook camera. Everyone trusted her with things they weren't ready to say out loud."

"You went to her."

"After I saw the morning paper say Daniel was missing. Before they found the body. I told Eleanor what I saw. She took me back to the quarry and photographed the road, the blanket, tire marks. Amos was there. He told her not to print anything until they knew who was in the water."

"Who was in the water?" Harrow asked.

"I don't know."

"You never asked?"

"I asked once. Eleanor told me to forget until forgetting became dangerous."

That sounded exactly like Mara's mother, which made it hurt more.

When the interview ended, Caleb was left in the room with water and a deputy outside the door. He was no longer cuffed to the table, but he looked less free than before.

Mara stepped into the hallway and found the station too bright.

Harrow came out behind her. "The scarf."

"Lydia."

"Maybe."

"Blue with white stitching."

"A lot of things are blue with white stitching."

"You don't believe that."

"I don't believe in skipping steps because a clue feels narratively satisfying."

Mara almost laughed. "Narratively satisfying?"

"I read."

Voss joined them with his notebook closed. "We need Lydia Frost."

"And Thomas Keene," Mara said. "And Nora. And the identity of Ruth."

"And the body from 1998," Harrow added. "If there is still evidence to test."

The station phone rang. Pike answered at the front desk, listened, and turned toward them.

"Sheriff. Alvarez says you need to come back to the church."

Harrow took the receiver. "Why?"

Mara watched her face as she listened. First irritation. Then attention. Then the stillness of a person rearranging a case.

"Don't touch it," Harrow said, and hung up.

"What?" Voss asked.

"Salt."

They drove the two blocks to St. Bartholomew's because Harrow had no patience for walking under public observation. The church remained taped and guarded, but the square had not emptied. Bellweather simply shifted its watching from one doorway to another.

Alvarez met them at the vestry entrance. "I was doing the stair photographs before casting the tool marks. You need to see the fifth step from the top."

They climbed carefully in shoe covers. Mara stayed last, counting steps by habit. The tower smelled colder now without Amos in it. His body had been removed, leaving a shape in the room more disturbing than presence.

Alvarez crouched near the top of the stair. "There."

On the fifth stone step from the ringing chamber, in a shallow worn crescent near the wall, lay a residue of pale crystals mixed with grit. Not much. Enough to catch light.

"Road salt?" Voss asked.

"Likely," Alvarez said. "But there's more. The grit includes crushed shell and a greenish mineral I don't usually see in street treatment."

Mara looked at Harrow. "Lake road?"

Harrow shook her head slowly. "Mill causeway."

The old mill causeway ran across the wetland north of town, where winter crews used a cheaper salt mix cut with crushed shell from the processing plant and green quarry sand from a supplier in Rockport. Everyone complained about it because it chewed through undercarriages and left pale crust on boots.

"Who came from the mill causeway last night?" Voss asked.

"Half the town could have," Harrow said. "But not by accident. The causeway's closed for storm washout."

Alvarez pointed to another mark, lower on the same step. "Partial tread. Deep lug, work boot, size maybe ten or eleven. Overlies the wet smear from the rope drip."

"Meaning after the rope was already wet," Mara said.

"Likely."

"After the bell rang?"

"Careful," Harrow said.

Alvarez nodded. "After water from the rope was on the step. That's all I can say."

Mara looked up toward the ringing chamber. "Someone came down with mill salt on their boots after the rope had been wet. If the rope got wet in the storm before or during the ringing, the person left late in the sequence."

"Or a responder brought it in," Voss said.

Harrow shook her head. "Pike came from home on Oak Street. I came from the station. Keene from the rectory. None of us crossed the causeway."

"Nora?" Mara asked.

"Bell House."

"Lydia?"

"Lives near the clinic, south side."

"Eliot Sayer?"

Harrow's expression changed.

Voss noticed. "What?"

"Sayer's temporary office is in the old mill."

Below them, the church door opened. A voice called up, muffled by the stair.

"Sheriff?"

It was Pike.

Harrow went to the stairwell. "What?"

"Owen Vale is gone."

Harrow closed her eyes for one second.

Pike continued, voice thin with dread. "He said he needed the bathroom. I had Deputy Marsh outside the window, but Owen must have gone through the ceiling panel. Marsh found his jacket in the alley."

Voss said, "Damn it."

Mara looked again at the salt crystals. Mill causeway. Eliot Sayer. Owen fleeing. Caleb's old statement. Lydia's scarf. The case was no longer a line; it was a wheel, and someone had put a hand on every spoke.

Harrow started down the stairs. "Find Owen before the town does."

Mara followed, thinking of Amos's ledger, the missing page, and the single instruction her mother had left inside an envelope already opened by another hand.

If Amos had died for the last page he wrote, Owen Vale might be running with the next one.

Chapter 9: The Minister's Key

Reverend Thomas Keene kept his keys on a brass ring shaped like a fish.

It was the kind of detail Mara would have ignored in another town. In Bellweather, symbols had a way of becoming alibis. Keene placed the ring on the interview table at the station with both hands, as though surrendering something sacramental rather than municipal.

"Church front, vestry, tower, office, records closet, parish hall, bell cabinet," Harrow said, touching each key with a capped pen while Pike photographed them. "Anyone else have copies?"

Keene sat very straight. He had changed from his damp clerical shirt into a gray sweater and collar, but the skin beneath his eyes looked bruised by lack of sleep. "The wardens have church keys. Amos had tower and bell cabinet keys. Nora Bell has a parish hall key for heritage committee events. The fire department has an emergency set."

"And the tower key?"

"Only Amos and me."

Harrow looked at him. "You told me last night the lock wouldn't turn."

"It would not."

"You tried once?"

"Several times."

"Then the key broke."

"After Deputy Pike asked me to try again."

Pike shifted near the wall. "Sheriff told me to get it open."

"No one's blaming you," Harrow said, in a tone suggesting blame remained available if needed. She turned back to Keene. "When did you last use the tower key before last night?"

"Sunday, before service. Amos thought the bell assist was sticking."

"Was it?"

"He said the action felt dirty."

Mara stood behind the one-way glass with Voss, watching. Harrow had allowed her that much after making her wait in the hallway for twenty minutes while they searched for Owen and failed. The station's observation room was really a storage closet with a reinforced window, two folding chairs, and a shelf full of obsolete breathalyzer parts. It smelled of dust and toner.

"Dirty," Voss repeated softly.

"Amos would say that about machinery," Mara said. "He meant it felt interfered with."

In the interview room, Harrow asked, "Did you inspect it?"

"No. I know bells spiritually, Sheriff, not mechanically."

"Who does know it mechanically?"

"Amos. Caleb Rusk, sometimes. The service company in Augusta, when we can afford them."

"Eliot Sayer?"

Keene blinked. "Mr. Sayer?"

"He has an office at the mill."

"The mill and the church are not connected."

"Answer the question."

"I have never seen Eliot Sayer work on the bell."

Harrow let that sit. "Did Amos speak to you about Daniel Bell?"

Keene's hands moved under the table. Mara could not see them, but she saw the shoulders tighten.

"Daniel was dead before I entered seminary."

"That's not what I asked."

"Then ask plainly."

"Did Amos speak to you about Daniel Bell in the weeks before his death?"

Keene lowered his eyes. "Yes."

Voss leaned closer to the glass.

"What did he say?" Harrow asked.

"That old sins prefer new clothes."

Harrow stared at him. "Did he say anything useful?"

"He asked whether confession binds the listener if the confessor is dead."

"Whose confession?"

"He would not say."

"Did you tell him?"

"I told him a pastor is not a court."

"That's convenient."

Keene's head came up. "It is not convenience. It is doctrine, and it is also sometimes mercy."

Harrow's voice cooled. "Mercy for whom?"

The minister did not answer.

Mara watched him. Keene had been at the quarry in 1998, not yet a minister, not yet hidden behind theological vocabulary. A boy, then a man, then a keeper of confessions in the same town where one confession had apparently lived long enough to kill.

"Ask about Lydia," Mara said.

Voss looked at her.

"Ask about the scarf," she said.

Voss opened the observation room door and murmured to Pike, who passed a note to Harrow. Harrow read it without visible reaction.

"Did Lydia Frost come to the church last night?" she asked.

Keene's gaze flicked toward the glass. Not much. Enough.

"No."

"Did you see her yesterday?"

"Bellweather is small."

"At the church?"

"She brought flowers for the altar guild."

"When?"

"Late afternoon."

"Was Amos there?"

"I don't know."

"You don't know whether the murdered bell-ringer was in the church while Lydia Frost was here?"

"Amos came and went. He had his own key."

"Did Lydia go into the tower?"

"No."

"You're sure?"

"I did not see her go."

"That is different."

Keene closed his eyes briefly. "Yes."

Harrow slid a photograph across the table. It was an evidence print of the cloth from Amos's hand: blue fabric, torn edge, white stitching. "Do you recognize this?"

For the first time, Keene looked afraid in a way that had nothing to do with police.

"It could be many things."

"That's the town motto today."

"It resembles a choir scarf."

"From St. Bartholomew's?"

"We used blue scarves for Advent in the youth choir years ago. They were stored in the records closet."

"Do you still have them?"

"I believe so."

"We'll need to see."

"Sheriff, those children are grown now. Some are dead. Some moved away."

"Fabric doesn't care."

Keene folded his hands on the table. "Amos objected to the scarves."

Harrow waited.

"He said the color was wrong. Too close to the old Bell House livery. He said the church should not dress children in a family's flag."

"When was this?"

"Late nineties."

Mara felt the years align: Daniel, Lydia's scarf on Caleb's jaw, Amos's objection, a scrap in a dead hand.

Harrow leaned forward. "Reverend, did you remove any records from the church after Amos died?"

"No."

"Did you remove records before?"

Keene's mouth opened, then closed.

There it was again: the small pause before the chosen lie.

"Tom," Harrow said, using his first name for the first time, "do not make me find it without you."

Keene looked toward the one-way glass as if he knew Mara stood behind it. "Amos gave me an envelope."

"When?"

"Yesterday evening."

"What was in it?"

"I did not open it."

"Where is it?"

"In my office safe."

Harrow stood so quickly the chair legs scraped. "Pike."

The minister raised a hand. "He told me if anything happened to him, I should give it to Eleanor Ellison."

Silence reached through the glass and put its hand on Mara's throat.

Harrow turned, slowly, toward the observation window.

Keene continued, voice thinning. "I told him Eleanor was dead. He said, 'Then God help us. Give it to the daughter, if she remembers how to listen.'"

Mara left the observation room before anyone could tell her to stay.

The safe in Keene's office was not a safe so much as a fire box with ambition, tucked behind a shelf of stewardship brochures and old Christmas pageant scripts. Alvarez photographed it, then Keene opened it with a combination his fingers entered from memory. Inside were insurance papers, baptism certificates awaiting filing, a velvet pouch of communion vessels, and a manila envelope addressed in Amos Vale's hand:

E. ELLISON / M. ELLISON IF NEEDED

Harrow bagged it before opening because discipline mattered most when impatience had good arguments. Back at the station, with Alvarez present and the chain of custody documented, she slit the envelope along one edge.

Inside was a photocopy of a photograph.

Four teenagers stood near the quarry road in the gray light of morning. Caleb was identifiable by the blood-dark cloth pressed to his jaw. Lydia Frost, younger and round-faced, stood beside him in a sleeveless dress, blue scarf missing from her neck. Thomas Keene knelt near a pile of wet papers. Nora Bell stood farther back, arms crossed, her face turned toward a figure at the edge of the frame.

The figure was wrapped in a horse blanket.

His face was blurred by motion, but the dark hair, the height, the angle of the shoulders matched Daniel Bell's graduation photograph too closely to dismiss.

Behind him, half in shadow beside Amos Vale's truck, stood another person in a hooded raincoat.

On the back of the photocopy, Amos had written:

The original shows the hand.

"Whose hand?" Voss asked.

Mara looked at the blurred hooded figure. Only one hand was visible, pale against the dark raincoat, resting on Daniel's arm.

On the ring finger was a square black stone set in silver.

Harrow said, "Do you recognize it?"

Mara did.

She had seen that ring in her mother's jewelry box after the funeral, wrapped in tissue and labeled in Eleanor's handwriting with a single word:

Borrowed.

Chapter 10: A Photograph Torn Twice

The ring was not in Eleanor Ellison's jewelry box when Mara opened it with a gloved hand and a witness at her shoulder.

That should have surprised her. It did not. The day had become a lesson in absence: missing ledger page, missing original photograph, missing Owen, missing ring. Bellweather's secrets did not vanish cleanly. They left outlines sharp enough to cut.

The jewelry box sat on Eleanor's dresser beneath the oval mirror Mara had hated as a teenager because it made her face look longer and her shoulders narrower. The box was cherry wood, lined in green velvet, organized with the same stern logic as everything else in the house. Brooches by type. Earrings paired and pinned through index cards. Necklaces coiled in tiny cloth bags. Mourning jewelry in a bottom drawer with a note explaining each dead relative because Eleanor had distrusted memory without labels.

The tissue that had held the square black ring remained in the back compartment.

Borrowed, the note said.

No date. No name.

Harrow stood beside the dresser, expression unreadable. Voss waited by the door. Alvarez photographed the box, the tissue, the note, the empty compartment.

"When did you last see the ring?" Harrow asked.

"After the funeral. I opened the box to find her wedding band for the service."

"Was the black ring there then?"

"Yes."

"You didn't mention it."

"It didn't mean anything."

"Now it does."

"Now it does," Mara agreed.

Harrow looked around the bedroom. It had not been obviously searched. The bed was made, curtains open, slippers aligned near the chair. Eleanor's robe hung on a hook behind the door, sleeves drooping with unbearable familiarity.

"Who had access to the house after the funeral?" Voss asked.

"Me. The estate lawyer. Caleb, for the roof estimate. Lydia brought food. June checked the mail before I arrived."

"Nora?"

"Not with permission."

Harrow wrote names. "Any spare keys?"

"Kitchen drawer. One missing when I checked yesterday, but I assumed I had taken it years ago."

"Did you?"

"I don't know."

The honest answer irritated Mara. She liked facts that had the decency to remain fixed.

Alvarez bagged the tissue and note. "There are fibers caught in the drawer seam."

She lifted them with tape: two threads, dark blue and white.

Harrow's face tightened. "Scarf?"

"Maybe. Could be anything."

"Everyone in this case should be forbidden to say that."

Mara looked at the bed. "Whoever opened the envelope in the study may have taken the ring."

"Why leave the note?" Voss asked.

"Because they were in a hurry. Or because they didn't know it mattered. Or because the note matters differently."

Borrowed.

The word made less sense the longer Mara looked at it. Eleanor labeled ownership. Her notes were precise: Aunt Mae's garnet, Grandma L's mourning pin, found in school desk 2004, Mara prom earrings. Borrowed was not ownership. It was a debt, a reminder, a promise to return.

"Borrowed from whom?" Harrow asked.

Mara saw again the hand in Amos's photocopy. The ring on the hooded figure. If Eleanor had possessed the ring, then either she had been the hooded figure in the photograph or she had later obtained the ring from whoever was.

Her stomach turned at both possibilities.

"We need the original," she said.

"Obviously."

"No. I mean Eleanor would not keep only a photocopy if the original showed the hand clearly."

Harrow studied her. "Where would she hide it?"

"Somewhere labeled as something else."

The search of Eleanor's house widened under the consent Mara had already given and the practical reality that refusing now would look like obstruction dressed as grief. They found three boxes of school photographs, two binders of town council minutes, one envelope of Daniel Bell clippings, and a locked metal recipe file in the pantry that contained no recipes.

Inside the recipe file were index cards organized by name.

BELL, ALISTAIR: payments to mill families, land transfer dates, Ruth?
BELL, DANIEL: quarry, July 3-5, alive? photograph, blanket, "bell lied."
BELL, NORA: protects father, knows more than says, ask about blue livery.
FROST/MARCH, LYDIA: scarf, nursing school departure, Ruth connection?
KEENE, THOMAS: confession? seminary after 1998.
RUSK, CALEB: statement recanted, jaw, guilt, useful but afraid.
VALE, AMOS: ledger, one bell July 4, keeps copies badly.

Mara read her mother's notes with the unpleasant intimacy of discovering a second life in the handwriting of the first. Eleanor had not been dabbling in town gossip. She had been building a case quietly for decades, adding pieces, withholding conclusion.

At the back of the file was a card for ELLISON, MARA.

Harrow looked at Mara before reading it aloud.

"Don't," Mara said.

Harrow handed it to her instead.

Mara turned the card over.

Mara asks too directly. Tell only when proof can protect her from certainty.

For a moment the pantry blurred. Mara put the card down carefully, as if care could substitute for composure.

Voss cleared his throat. "There are two photographs stuck behind the file liner."

Alvarez removed the liner with a knife. The first photograph was a grainy print of the quarry road at dawn, likely the source of Amos's photocopy. It had been torn in half and taped back together once, then torn again through the hooded figure's face. The hand remained visible. The ring was sharp.

The second photograph showed the old mill at night. A young woman stood near the loading dock with one hand over her mouth. Beside her, half-turned from the camera, was Alistair Bell. In the background, a teenage Daniel Bell faced them both, satchel in hand.

On the back, Eleanor had written:

Ruth March, 1979. Lydia's mother.

Harrow read the name twice. "March."

"Lydia's maiden name," Mara said.

"Ruth connection," Voss murmured.

"Daniel said, 'Ask Ruth what you bought with her silence,'" Mara said. "Caleb remembered it."

Harrow's phone rang. She listened, then put it on speaker.

"Sheriff," Pike said. "We found Owen's truck."

"Where?"

"Mill causeway. Empty. Door open. Blood on the driver's seat."

No one in Eleanor's pantry moved.

Pike continued, "And Sheriff? The bell rope from the old mill siren is missing."

Before they left for the mill, Harrow ordered every loose paper in Eleanor's study photographed in place. That took time. Time, in murder work, was both enemy and alibi: the thing killers used and investigators were accused of wasting. Mara stood in the hallway while Alvarez moved through the study with a camera, and the house shifted around her under the weight of strangers.

Eleanor had kept a narrow table by the hall window. On it sat a blue glass bowl where she dropped keys, stamps, buttons that had lost their garments, and coins too sticky for purses. Mara had once hidden a failed math test beneath that bowl, believing its transparency decorative rather than functional. Eleanor had waited until dinner to say, "Concealment works poorly through glass," and signed the test without further lecture. The phrase returned now with such force that Mara had to put one hand against the wall.

Concealment works poorly through glass.

Unless everyone agrees not to look.

Voss came from the kitchen carrying a plastic evidence pouch. "You okay?"

"That question has lost useful scale."

"Fair." He held up the pouch. Inside was a Polaroid of Eleanor's back garden in winter, nothing obvious except snow, the maple tree, the shed, and a dark rectangle near the fence. "This was tucked behind the pantry clock."

"Date?"

"No stamp. But the shed roof matches the repair Caleb did last winter."

Mara looked closer. The dark rectangle near the fence was not a shadow. It was the back gate standing open. In the snow, two sets of tracks crossed the garden: one from the kitchen door to the shed, another from the side alley to the gate.

"Someone visited her."

"Looks that way."

"Caleb?"

"Maybe. Shoe size estimate from photo is useless. But the alley tracks are narrow."

Mara thought of Lydia's clinic shoes, Nora's polished boots, Keene's careful steps, June's cane, Amos's old gait. Everyone in town had feet. That was the problem with physical evidence before context.

Voss slid the pouch into a box. "Your mother photographed intrusion?"

"My mother photographed everything that annoyed her."

"Useful habit."

"Ask my childhood."

He almost smiled, then stopped at the expression on her face. "We will treat Eleanor's house as connected."

"It is connected."

"More connected."

"That is not comforting."

"It was not meant to be."

In the study, Harrow opened a desk drawer and found a packet of index cards tied with gray thread. She glanced toward Mara before reading, which was becoming a kindness Mara disliked because it was needed.

The top card read:

IF THEY COME AFTER ME, START WITH WHO NEEDS DELAY, NOT SILENCE.

Mara took one breath and then another.

Delay, not silence. Eleanor had understood the difference. Silence buried; delay bought time; delay could look like mercy, procedure, weather, illness, a private settlement, a misplaced key. Amos had been delayed. Daniel had been delayed for twenty-eight years. Ruth's deed had been delayed into history. Matthew's body had been delayed into the wrong grave. Eleanor herself had been delayed by a stroke that might have been natural and might have been helped along by people who only needed her unavailable.

Harrow bagged the card. "We add it to the board."

"Which board?"

"At this point? The wall."

Mara looked around the study. "She knew she was in danger."

"Maybe."

"Dina."

Harrow closed the drawer. "Knowing danger is not the same as knowing who brings it. Your mother seems to have known the whole town was a loaded weapon. That does not tell me whose finger was on the trigger."

It was true. Mara resented it anyway.

Outside, a siren started toward the mill causeway, thin at first, then widening. The house seemed to release them. Mara followed Harrow out, but at the door she looked back into the hall. The blue glass bowl caught gray light from the window. Nothing was hidden under it now.

That felt less like order than warning.

Chapter 11: Harrow Draws a Line

Harrow drove to the mill with her lights off and her anger visible only in the speedometer.

Mara sat in the passenger seat because Harrow had pointed at the cruiser and said, "In," with the tone of a woman choosing the least bad version of a mistake. Voss followed with Alvarez in the county sedan. Pike remained at the station to explain to Owen Vale's increasingly hysterical mother that her son was not under arrest, not free, not safe, and not yet found.

The old mill stood north of town where Bellweather River narrowed before entering the lake. Once, it had produced wool blankets, uniforms, and the kind of local prosperity people still used as proof of character generations after the paychecks stopped. Now the brick buildings sagged behind chain-link fencing, windows boarded, smokestack cracked, weeds growing through loading bays. Eliot Sayer's company had hung a banner over the main gate: BELLWEATHER LANDING - HERITAGE RESIDENCES, MARKET HALL, WATERFRONT WALK.

Someone had slashed HERITAGE with black paint.

The causeway approach was closed by orange barricades and a sign reading WASHOUT - NO THROUGH TRAFFIC. Harrow stopped before the barricade. Owen's truck sat twenty yards beyond, nose angled toward the marsh, driver's door open. Mud streaked the tires. White salt crust marked the running board.

"Stay by the car," Harrow said.

"Dina."

"I mean it."

"If Owen is bleeding and moving, time matters."

"If Owen is bait, time matters differently."

That was true enough to silence Mara for five seconds.

Harrow got out. Mara followed to the front of the cruiser but stopped there. She watched Harrow and Voss approach the truck slowly, hands clear, eyes moving. Alvarez began photographing from the barricade. Wind pushed marsh grass flat in silver waves. The mill loomed beyond them, every broken window an unblinking eye.

Harrow looked into the truck and swore.

"Blood?" Mara called.

Harrow turned. "Stay there."

That answer was enough.

Mara scanned the causeway. Tire tracks overlapped in mud: Owen's truck, a wider vehicle, perhaps a van, and older construction ruts. Footprints led from the driver's side toward the mill gate, but rain and marsh water had softened them into suggestions. Near the open truck door, something blue clung to a thorny weed.

"Harrow," Mara said.

"Do not cross the tape I have not yet had time to put up."

"Blue fabric, two o'clock from the driver's door, on the weed."

Alvarez pivoted her camera. Harrow saw it and motioned her forward.

The scrap was darker than the cloth from Amos's hand, soaked through, but it had a line of pale stitching along one edge.

"Could be planted," Voss said.

"Everything can be planted," Harrow snapped. "That doesn't make evidence decorative."

Mara looked at the mill. "Where is Sayer's office?"

"West annex," Harrow said. "Temporary trailer inside the fence."

"He there?"

"He says he was in Portland last night."

"Says?"

"Left a message with dispatch before dawn offering full cooperation from Portland."

"Convenient distance."

Harrow walked back toward Mara. The closer she came, the less controlled she looked. Not panicked. Worse: determined to cut away distractions, and Mara knew she was one.

"You are done," Harrow said.

"No."

"That wasn't a question."

"Owen's truck has mill salt, blood, and blue fabric. The bell rope from the old siren is missing. Amos died in a bell tower. Caleb says Daniel said the bell lied. My mother's house was entered, evidence removed, and a ring in her possession connects to the photograph. You cannot put me outside this by saying done."

"I can put you outside my scene."

"This is not only your scene."

Harrow stepped close enough that Mara could see rain caught in her eyelashes. "That is exactly the problem. Every time I turn around, your mother is in my evidence, your grief is in my hallway, your old job is in my ear, and your certainty is trying to drive."

Mara said nothing.

"I need you to understand me," Harrow continued. "I will use what you know. I will take your statements. I will search Eleanor's papers lawfully. But you are not law enforcement here. You are a witness. You may be connected to missing evidence. You may be connected to a twenty-seven-year cover-up through your mother. If I let you keep standing beside me, a defense attorney will build a house inside that mistake and live there rent-free."

Voss looked away, which told Mara he agreed.

The words were correct. That made them worse.

"Owen is missing," Mara said.

"And I have deputies, county, and state assistance coming."

"State?"

"Yes. I called them."

There it was: the door closing.

Mara looked past Harrow to the truck. "If you push me out now, people will stop talking to you and start talking around you."

"Then tell them to stop."

"They won't."

"Then be useful at home. Make a list of everyone who had a key to Eleanor's house. Write down every memory of Daniel Bell, every mention your mother made of Ruth March, every time Amos contacted her. Do it on paper. Date it. Sign it. Give it to me. That is how you help without burning the case down."

Mara hated her for giving good instructions.

Wind moved across the marsh. Somewhere inside the mill, a loose sheet of metal banged once.

All four of them turned.

Harrow raised a hand. Voss unclipped his weapon. Alvarez stepped back. The sound came again, hollow and irregular. Metal in wind, probably. Or someone moving through the old loading bay.

Harrow pointed at Mara, then at the cruiser. "Inside. Now."

This time Mara obeyed. Not because she accepted the line, but because Harrow was about to enter a dangerous structure and did not need the burden of watching her disobey.

From the cruiser, Mara watched Harrow and Voss move toward the gate. Alvarez called for backup. The mill swallowed the two officers through a side door near the west annex.

Minutes passed.

Mara's phone buzzed.

Unknown number.

She answered.

For three seconds there was only breathing and static. Then a man's voice, low and strained.

"Mara Ellison?"

"Who is this?"

"Your mother said you would ask the wrong first question."

Mara sat very still.

"Where is Owen?" she said.

"Alive, if they hurry. Not if they go through the front."

"Who is this?"

"Ask Nora what she did with Daniel's satchel."

"Daniel?"

A pause.

Then the voice said, "No. Daniel is what they called me before I died."

The line went dead.

Mara opened the cruiser door.

Chapter 12: The Mill Offer

Mara did not run toward the mill. Running would draw Alvarez, and Alvarez would stop her because Alvarez still believed in lines painted by authority. Mara walked fast along the barricade, phone in hand, eyes on the west annex door where Harrow and Voss had disappeared.

"Ms. Ellison," Alvarez called. "Sheriff said in the car."

"Owen may be inside and the front entrance may be trapped."

That changed the air faster than argument could have. Alvarez lowered her camera. "Source?"

"Anonymous call. Male. Claimed Daniel Bell is alive."

Alvarez's skepticism was immediate and appropriate. "Stay where you are."

"Tell Harrow on the radio."

Alvarez keyed her shoulder mic. "Sheriff, possible warning from Ellison. Anonymous caller says do not enter through front, Owen alive inside, possible trap."

Static answered.

"Sheriff, copy?"

Nothing.

The mill banged again, louder.

Alvarez looked at the building, then at Mara. "Do not move."

She moved toward the gate, radio raised. Mara counted to three and followed at a distance that preserved the illusion of restraint. The chain-link gate was locked with a heavy padlock, but the pedestrian gap beside it had been cut and folded back, a flap of wire just wide enough for a person. Fresh rust marked the bend. People had been entering the mill long before murder gave them permission.

Inside the fence, the ground turned to gravel and weeds. Construction survey flags snapped in the wind. The temporary office trailer sat near the west annex with SAYER DEVELOPMENT printed on its side in tasteful navy letters. Its windows were dark. Beside it, a black SUV gleamed with out-of-place cleanliness.

"Eliot's back," Mara said.

Alvarez glanced at the SUV. "You know his vehicle?"

"No. I know his taste."

The trailer door opened.

Eliot Sayer stepped out wearing a camel overcoat, polished boots, and the weary expression of a man inconvenienced by other people's emergencies. He was in his late fifties, handsome in a boardroom way, with silver hair and a face arranged around permanent mild concern. He held a phone in one hand.

"Officer," he called. "I was told to meet Sheriff Harrow here."

Alvarez kept her distance. "Stay where you are."

"Of course."

Mara stopped near a stack of pallets. Sayer saw her and frowned with practiced politeness.

"Mara Ellison," he said. "I'm sorry about your mother."

"Everyone is."

He accepted the rebuff as if it were a canapé he had chosen not to eat. "Eleanor was a remarkable woman."

"You knew her?"

"Everyone knew her."

"That's what people say when they didn't."

His smile thinned.

Alvarez spoke into her radio again. Still no answer from Harrow. Sirens sounded faintly from town: backup, late and gathering speed.

Mara looked at the trailer. "You said you were in Portland."

Sayer turned back to her. "I was."

"Fast drive."

"I left immediately when I heard Owen Vale's truck had been found on my property."

"Your property?"

"Under contract."

"Not yet yours."

"A distinction my attorneys would respect more than I do at the moment."

Mara stepped closer despite Alvarez's warning look. "Why does your development deal matter to Amos Vale?"

"I can't imagine it did."

"He had a deed."

Sayer's eyes changed. Not much. Men like him learned to keep reaction behind expensive manners, but surprise still had to pass through the body before discipline caught it.

"A great many obsolete papers circulate in towns like this," he said.

"Obsolete enough to kill for?"

"Ms. Ellison, grief has made you theatrical."

"Bellweather is theatrical. I'm adapting."

Alvarez moved between them slightly. "Mr. Sayer, when did you arrive?"

"Twenty minutes ago."

"From Portland?"

"Yes."

"Anyone confirm?"

"My assistant, hotel valet, turnpike records, phone GPS, whatever else satisfies the modern appetite for surveillance."

Mara watched his boots. Polished leather, dark soles, no obvious salt crust. Too clean for a storm, too clean for a mill, too clean perhaps because he had changed them.

"Where are your work boots?" she asked.

Sayer looked down, then back at her. "I beg your pardon?"

"The causeway is mud and salt. You own a development site. You have work boots."

"In the trailer, I assume."

"Assume?"

"I don't inventory my footwear under questioning by civilians."

The west annex door burst open. Voss came out first, coughing, one arm around Owen Vale.

Owen's face was gray. Blood darkened his hairline and collar. His hands were tied in front of him with a length of old rope. Harrow followed, limping, weapon drawn toward the darkness behind them.

"Ambulance!" Voss shouted.

Alvarez ran. Sayer stepped back sharply. Mara stayed where she was because Harrow's gun remained fixed on the doorway.

Inside the annex, something scraped across concrete.

"Show me your hands!" Harrow called.

No one emerged.

Voss lowered Owen to the gravel. Owen coughed and tried to speak. Mara knelt beside him before anyone stopped her.

"Who did this?" she asked.

His eyes rolled toward her. "Bell."

"Nora?"

"No." He swallowed. "The dead one."

Then he passed out.

The ambulance took Owen. Harrow refused medical attention for herself until Voss threatened to have her medically overruled in front of every deputy arriving from three jurisdictions. Her limp came from a fall through a rotten section of flooring just inside the annex. Voss had caught her belt and kept her from going fully into the old dye pit below. Owen had been tied to a support column thirty feet inside, gagged, bleeding from a scalp wound, and surrounded by enough loose timber that entering from the front loading bay would likely have brought half the mezzanine down.

"Trap?" Mara asked.

Harrow sat on the ambulance bumper while a paramedic cleaned her shin.

"Neglect weaponized," Harrow said. "Someone knew the building."

"Daniel called me."

Harrow's gaze lifted. "You don't know that."

"The caller said Daniel is what they called him before he died."

"People can say things."

"Owen said the dead Bell did this."

"Owen has a head injury."

"And you have a suspect pretending to be in Portland while standing beside a clean SUV at the mill."

Sayer stood twenty yards away speaking to Voss, all concern and cooperation. He had removed his overcoat and draped it over one arm. His suit beneath was charcoal, immaculate. Around his wrist flashed a watch worth more than most Bellweather roofs.

"Sayer didn't tie Owen up while we were standing here," Harrow said.

"No. But his deal touches every piece. The mill, Bell land, disputed deeds, Daniel's claim if he's alive."

"If."

"You heard Owen."

"I heard a concussed man say a dead person hurt him."

Mara looked toward the mill. "What did you find inside?"

"Owen. Rope. Blood. Fresh boot prints. Old siren mechanism missing its pull rope."

"Same rope used on Owen?"

"Maybe. Lab will tell us."

"Was the rope blue?"

"No. Don't start."

Mara almost did. Instead she looked at Sayer again.

Voss ended his conversation and came over. "Mr. Sayer says he received an email from Owen at eleven thirty last night."

Harrow straightened. "Saying?"

"That Owen had proof Amos Vale was hiding a deed that could stop the mill sale. He wanted fifty thousand dollars."

"Blackmail," Harrow said.

"Attempted. Sayer says he ignored it until this morning."

"Does he have the email?"

"Forwarded it."

Mara watched Sayer's face as he spoke to a deputy. Calm. Saddened. Slightly offended by physical decay around him. "Where would Owen get proof?"

"Amos," Harrow said.

"Or the missing ledger page."

Voss looked at her. "Maybe that's why Owen ran."

Harrow stood, testing her leg. "We search the trailer."

Sayer objected with the precision of a man who had expected to object and prepared language. Harrow obtained consent only after explaining that refusal would hold the property under guard until a warrant arrived and that the optics of delaying a search while a bleeding man was removed from the premises might trouble even his attorneys.

The trailer was warm, dry, and scented faintly with cedar. Architectural renderings covered one wall: brick mill reborn as luxury lofts, families strolling along a boardwalk where marsh grass currently hid rusted scrap, a market hall glowing with fictional community. On the desk lay contracts, survey maps, a laptop, and a leather folio.

In the bottom drawer, behind rolled blueprints, Alvarez found a pair of work boots.

The soles were wet.

Salt crusted the treads in pale ridges, mixed with crushed shell and greenish grit.

Sayer looked at the boots and said nothing.

Mara looked instead at the wall rendering. In the artist's version, the old bell tower of St. Bartholomew's appeared in the distance, picturesque and small, reduced to ambiance for people who would pay to live above a town whose history had not yet finished killing.

They did not leave the trailer immediately. Harrow made Sayer sit in the one clean guest chair while Alvarez photographed the boots, the drawer, the desktop, the map tubes, the laptop, the tasteful pen set engraved with his company name. Men like Sayer bought objects that said order even when standing in rooms built over rot.

"Those boots are used by site staff," Sayer said.

"Staff with your shoe size?" Voss asked.

"I do not know the shoe sizes of my employees."

Mara stood near the renderings and looked at what the future had been made to erase. In one image, the mill's west annex was a restaurant with Edison bulbs and exposed brick, the sort of industrial nostalgia that required no one to remember industrial injury. In another, the marsh was crossed by a boardwalk populated by people in linen who had never smelled low tide. The church tower appeared in three separate views, always far enough away to be charm and never close enough to be evidence.

"You kept the tower in the drawings," Mara said.

Sayer turned his head. "The church is part of the town's visual identity."

"So was the mill payroll."

"I restore buildings, Ms. Ellison. I do not restore economic systems."

"Convenient boundary."

His smile thinned. "All boundaries are convenient to someone."

Harrow looked up from the evidence table. "Careful, Mr. Sayer. You are drifting toward honesty."

He folded his hands in his lap. "Sheriff, I understand local suspicion of outside capital. But this project would bring jobs, tax revenue, preservation-"

"Do not sell me condos while I am bagging your boots."

For the first time, real irritation broke his polish. "Those boots prove only that someone at a construction site walked through site material."

"The site was closed."

"People trespass."

"People also murder."

Sayer's gaze moved to Mara as if she were the less official danger. "Your mother understood old towns cannot survive by embalming themselves."

The use of Eleanor's name changed the trailer's pressure. Harrow noticed. Voss noticed. Sayer noticed that they noticed and regretted nothing.

"You spoke with my mother," Mara said.

"Briefly."

"About what?"

"Historical context."

"That is a developer's phrase for finding which facts are load-bearing."

"She was knowledgeable. Guarded. Hostile to speculation."

"She disliked people who mistook curiosity for entitlement."

"She kept records that could materially affect a lawful transaction."

"There it is."

Harrow stepped between them, not physically, but with voice. "When did you last speak with Eleanor Ellison?"

"May ninth."

"Where?"

"Her house."

Mara said, "You came to my mother's house?"

"She invited me."

"No."

Sayer looked at her. "You cannot know that."

"I can know my mother."

"Can you?" he asked softly.

Voss moved half a step forward. Harrow's eyes hardened.

Sayer lifted one hand in apology too elegant to be sincere. "I mean only that parents often keep civic matters from children who have left."

"And what civic matter brought you to her study?"

"The school land transfers. The quarry conservation easement. The mill cottages. She had opinions."

"Did she have documents?"

"She said she had memory."

"That would have annoyed you."

"Memory does not cloud title. Documents do."

Mara looked at the boots again. "Did Amos have documents?"

Sayer said nothing.

Harrow smiled without warmth. "That one you choose not to answer?"

"I am choosing to wait for counsel."

"Excellent instinct. Late, but excellent."

Outside, the ambulance pulled away with Owen. Through the trailer window, Mara saw the mill yard swarm with uniforms and yellow tape. The old building seemed less abandoned now than awakened and displeased. It had held too many versions of work: wool, fraud, surveillance, coercion, hiding. The development renderings on the wall had failed to capture that.

Alvarez opened the laptop only far enough to confirm it required a password and then bagged it. In the desk's upper drawer she found a folder labeled COMMUNITY CONCERNS. Inside were printed profiles of Bellweather residents likely to oppose the project: June Pritchard, Eleanor Ellison, Amos Vale, Lydia Frost, Caleb Rusk, Reverend Keene, and Mara, though Mara had not lived in town for years. Her profile was thin but accurate: former state police, disciplinary leave after shooting review, resigned before internal promotion track collapsed, mother deceased, likely emotionally volatile around local matters.

Mara read it once.

Harrow took it gently from her hand. "Evidence."

"He profiled me before I arrived."

"Greer profiled you," Voss said.

Sayer's eyes moved. There, Mara thought. Greer before Greer had a name in the case.

"Who is Greer?" she asked.

Sayer's face closed.

Harrow looked at Voss. "Write that down."

"Already did."

The folder also contained a page on Amos:

VALE, AMOS. Elderly. Archivist tendencies. Holds informal records. Possible leverage: nephew's debts, church maintenance funding, reputation as eccentric. Risk: sympathetic if harmed. Best approach: discredit memory / isolate documents.

Harrow's expression went flat. "Risk: sympathetic if harmed."

Sayer said, "I have never seen that document."

"It was in your desk."

"This is a shared site office."

"Your name is on the door."

"A fact increasingly regrettable."

Mara looked at Amos's profile again. Best approach: discredit memory / isolate documents. The murder had not discredited Amos; it had made him central. That meant either the plan failed catastrophically, or someone used Sayer's private pressure campaign and turned it into a bell loud enough no developer could control.

"You were afraid of him alive," she said. "Someone else needed him dead."

Sayer gave her a strange look then, not gratitude, not fear. Recognition, perhaps. The kind one strategist gives another across a ruined board.

"Ms. Ellison," he said, "small towns often produce moral drama from ordinary leverage. Be careful not to confuse the two."

Mara almost stepped toward him.

Harrow said, "Mara."

The warning held. Barely.

As they left the trailer, Caleb was standing near the gate, rain dark on his jacket, watching Sayer through the window.

"What?" Mara asked.

"He came to my shop last month."

"Sayer?"

"His project manager. Bragg. Wanted a quote for restoring mill interior woodwork. Asked strange questions."

"Like?"

"Whether old mortise locks could be made to fail without looking forced. Whether antique bell hardware had resale value. Whether I knew who maintained St. Bart's tower."

Mara turned toward Harrow, who had heard enough to stop walking.

Caleb looked embarrassed, then angry at himself for embarrassment. "I thought he was making conversation."

Harrow said, "No one in this case gets to use that phrase anymore."

Chapter 13: Funeral Weather

Amos Vale's funeral was held before his body had cooled in public imagination.

Technically, it was a memorial vigil. Reverend Keene made that distinction twice from the pulpit and once to a television reporter from Portland who kept calling the church "picturesque" until Sheriff Harrow asked whether he also used that word for active homicide scenes. The actual burial would wait for the medical examiner, county paperwork, Owen Vale's consent if he recovered enough to give it, and the kind of bureaucratic decency murder denied.

Bellweather came anyway.

By dusk, St. Bartholomew's was full. People stood along the side aisles and beneath the choir loft. Rain returned in a fine, cold mist that silvered coats and made the stone steps shine. The bell did not ring. Its silence drew more attention than any tolling could have.

Mara sat in the back pew because she had learned that leaving required position. June Pritchard sat beside her, cane across her knees. Caleb Rusk stood at the rear wall under the watch of Deputy Pike, not arrested, not free from suspicion, not welcome enough to sit. Lydia Frost occupied a pew near the front with the altar guild, face pale above a navy coat. Nora Bell sat in the first row as if birthright included proximity to all public grief. Eliot Sayer did not attend, which was wise or cowardly depending on one's tolerance for theater.

Harrow stood near the side door, scanning the congregation. Her limp was barely visible, which meant she was hiding pain aggressively. Voss sat two pews ahead with his notebook closed on his knee.

Reverend Keene preached from Ecclesiastes and did not mention murder. He spoke of appointed seasons, of bells as instruments not only of announcement but remembrance, of Amos's devotion to "the ordinary holiness of keeping track." His voice steadied as he went. He was good at this, Mara thought. Whatever else he had hidden, he knew how to give sorrow a shape people could carry out of a room.

"Amos believed no honest sound fully disappears," Keene said.

Mara looked up sharply.

It was the line Amos had told her as a child.

June's hand found her sleeve. "Breathe," she murmured.

After the service, people formed clusters instead of leaving. Bellweather did not disperse grief until it had compared versions. Casseroles appeared in the parish hall. Coffee urns hissed. A table filled with photographs of Amos: young in a Navy uniform, middle-aged beside his wife Clara, elderly atop a ladder hanging Christmas greens, always thinner than the world around him, always looking slightly amused by human inefficiency.

Mara stood before one photograph showing Amos, Eleanor, and June at a school fundraiser sometime in the early 2000s. Eleanor wore a red sweater and held a clipboard like a weapon. Amos looked toward her with an expression Mara had never seen directed at her mother: trust complicated by worry.

"He loved her, you know."

Lydia Frost stood beside Mara with a paper cup of coffee untouched in both hands. Up close, Lydia looked exhausted. She had the practical posture of a nurse, weight balanced, eyes trained to notice deterioration. Her blond hair was pulled into a severe knot. A gold cross hung at her throat.

"Everyone loved my mother today," Mara said. "It must have been convenient for her after death."

Lydia flinched. "I deserved that less than some."

"Did you?"

The nurse looked at the photograph. "Eleanor scared me. That is not the same as disliking her."

"Why did she scare you?"

"Because she remembered what frightened people said after they had time to become respectable."

Mara turned fully. "Like what you said in 1998?"

Lydia's cup trembled. Coffee touched the rim but did not spill.

"This is Amos's vigil."

"Yes. And Amos died holding blue cloth."

Lydia closed her eyes briefly. "I gave Caleb my scarf because he was bleeding."

"Did you get it back?"

"No."

"Where did it go?"

"I thought Caleb kept it."

Across the hall, Caleb was speaking to June under Pike's impatient stare. Mara watched him laugh once at something June said, then stop as if laughter had surprised him.

"Caleb says you wrapped his jaw," Mara said. "He also says Alistair hit Daniel and Daniel ran."

Lydia's face tightened around old weather. "Caleb remembers what guilt lets him."

"What do you remember?"

"Rain. Daniel shouting. Nora crying without making sound. Thomas praying with mud on his knees. Alistair telling us that if another person went into the quarry, their blood would be on our hands."

"Was Daniel alive the next morning?"

Lydia looked at her then. "If he was, what does that make the body?"

"A question."

"Bodies are not questions to nurses."

"They are to investigators."

Lydia gave a tired little laugh. "You sound like Eleanor."

"Did my mother have Daniel's ring?"

"Daniel didn't wear a black ring."

The answer came too fast.

Mara said, "I didn't say Daniel did."

Lydia set down her coffee. "I need to help in the kitchen."

Mara caught her wrist lightly. Not enough to restrain, enough to ask whether Lydia would make a scene. The nurse looked at Mara's hand until Mara released her.

"Amos thought someone was poisoning him," Mara said.

Lydia went still.

"He told you?"

"He told someone."

"Old men on medication often fear medication."

"Did he ask you?"

"He asked me whether blood pressure pills could make a person confused."

"Could they?"

"Not his, not at his dose."

"Did you check?"

"I am not his doctor."

"You are a nurse."

"And this town uses nurses as confessionals, mechanics, pharmacists, and unpaid daughters. That does not mean I am responsible for every fear an old man brings me."

It was the first honest anger Lydia had shown. Mara respected it more than the tremor.

"Who prescribed his medication?"

"Dr. Anik at the clinic."

"Who filled it?"

"Bellweather Pharmacy."

"Who picked it up?"

Lydia's eyes flicked toward the front of the hall.

Nora stood near the photograph table, accepting condolences beside Owen's empty place. Her face was composed but watchful.

"Nora?" Mara asked.

"Sometimes," Lydia said. "Amos didn't drive much after his cataract surgery. People helped."

"Did you?"

"Sometimes."

"Did anyone have access to his pill organizer?"

"Anyone in his cottage could."

Before Mara could press, Reverend Keene approached. "Lydia, Mrs. Pritchard is asking for you."

June was not. She was still with Caleb, gesturing with her cane toward a photograph. Keene knew Mara knew that. His eyes asked her to let Lydia go.

"Of course," Lydia said, and left too quickly.

Mara looked at Keene. "You interrupted."

"Yes."

"Why?"

"Because grief makes poor interview rooms."

"Murder makes worse ones."

Keene lowered his voice. "Lydia has carried 1998 badly."

"So has everyone. Some people are dead from it."

"You think she killed Amos?"

"I think Amos died holding fabric like hers."

"Like half the choir's."

"Did you give Harrow the scarf inventory?"

"Yes."

"Any missing?"

He hesitated. "Three."

"From when?"

"No one inventoried them between 1999 and this morning."

Mara smiled without humor. "Convenient."

"No," Keene said. "Human."

The parish hall doors opened, letting in cold mist and Deputy Marsh. He went straight to Harrow and whispered. Harrow's head turned toward Mara.

Mara excused herself from Keene and crossed the hall.

"What?" she asked.

Harrow did not waste time telling her she had no right to ask. "Owen woke up. Says Amos told him he had proof Daniel Bell was alive. Says he stole a page from Amos's ledger after finding Amos dead."

"After?"

"Claims he found the tower door already open before the bell started. Panicked, took the page, ran, then came back when the bell rang with everyone else."

"That's absurd."

"Yes."

"Is it absurd because he's lying or because it changes the timeline?"

Harrow looked toward the silent tower. "Both."

Chapter 14: Lydia's Prescription

Bellweather Pharmacy occupied a narrow storefront between the hardware store and the bakery, with a green awning, old-fashioned gold lettering, and a bell above the door that rang too cheerfully for the morning after a vigil. The owner, Martin Pell, had been trying to retire for eleven years and had instead acquired a blood pressure cuff, a lottery terminal, and the resigned expression of a man who knew everyone's private rash.

He saw Harrow enter and closed the prescription window with a slide of frosted glass.

"No," Harrow said.

Martin opened it again.

Mara stood behind the greeting-card rack with Voss, officially present as the owner of Eleanor's house for a follow-up question that had not yet been asked. Unofficially, Harrow had stopped pretending Mara could be kept away from facts that were already walking into her kitchen at night.

"Amos Vale's medications," Harrow said. "We need fill records and pickup signatures for the last six months."

Martin pushed his glasses higher. "Warrant?"

Harrow placed the paper on the counter.

"Ah," Martin said. "Then yes."

While he gathered records, Mara studied the store. Bellweather Pharmacy sold cough drops, postcards, fishing licenses, reading glasses, local honey, sympathy cards, and exactly one brand of expensive face cream that Nora Bell had once convinced Martin tourists expected. A corkboard near the door held notices: lost cat, piano lessons, zoning hearing, church supper canceled, blood drive, mill redevelopment forum.

Someone had pinned a photocopy of Amos's obituary at the center.

Below it, in blue ink, someone had written: WHO RANG FOR AMOS?

Voss photographed it with his phone before Harrow told him to.

Martin returned with a folder. "Amos had lisinopril, atorvastatin, low-dose aspirin, eye drops after surgery, and occasional sleep medication prescribed last winter. Nothing exotic."

"Pickup signatures?" Harrow asked.

He handed over stapled receipts. "We don't always make the older folks sign if we know them."

Harrow gave him a look.

"Yes, yes, policy exists to be ignored by people who then regret it."

Mara leaned over the receipts. Names appeared in different hands: Amos Vale, Lydia Frost, Nora Bell, Caleb Rusk, T. Keene. Community care, or shared access. In Bellweather, the difference depended on who died.

"Sleep medication?" Voss asked.

"Zolpidem. Ten tablets. January. No refill."

"Any recent early refills? Lost pills?"

"No."

Harrow tapped a March receipt. "This one has initials only. L.F."

"Lydia Frost."

"You remember?"

"She picked up for half the old men in town when the clinic bus stopped running."

Mara noticed a receipt folded differently from the rest. "What about this one?"

Martin adjusted his glasses. "Amos's lisinopril. Picked up last week."

"Signature says N.B."

"Nora Bell."

"Why would Nora pick up his medication?"

Martin looked uncomfortable. "She was already here. Said Amos asked."

"Did he?"

"I didn't call to check. People help people, Sheriff."

"People also kill people, Martin."

He closed his mouth.

Voss asked, "Any chance pills could be swapped before pickup?"

Martin's offense returned usefully. "Not by me."

"After pickup?"

"Anyone with the bottle."

Harrow bagged copies of the records. "I need the dispensing logs."

"Those are electronic."

"Then print them."

As Martin retreated, Lydia Frost entered carrying a canvas tote and stopped when she saw them. For one second she looked like a woman who had walked into a room where her name had just been spoken, because she had.

"Sheriff," she said.

"Lydia."

"I came for clinic supplies."

"We'll need to speak with you."

"Again?"

"Yes."

Lydia's gaze moved to Mara. "Of course."

They used the vaccination room because it had a door. Posters about flu shots and tick prevention lined the walls. Lydia sat on the exam chair without being asked, hands folded around the tote strap.

Harrow began gently enough. "Amos told you he feared being poisoned."

"He feared being made confused."

"By medication?"

"At first."

"And later?"

Lydia looked at the sharps container, then back. "Later he asked whether small amounts of sedative could be placed into another pill bottle."

"What did you say?"

"Yes."

"Did he say why he asked?"

"He said he woke in the tower and did not remember climbing there."

Mara felt the room narrow. "When?"

Harrow glanced at her but did not stop the question.

Lydia answered, "Two weeks ago."

"Why didn't you tell us?"

"Because I told him to call his doctor, and because old people fall asleep in chairs and wake confused. Because Amos liked drama in small, archival doses. Because I did not think someone would kill him."
